I closed my hand around the stone. The smoothness was an insult. It offered no purchase. My mother had left it on a counter, a piece of a world she took for granted. A trivial beauty. It was not a tool. It was not a weapon. It was weight. I had carried it from a dying world into a dead void because I could not bear to delete one beautiful, useless thing.

I looked from the pulsing green beacon to the black shape of the station, a angular scar against the starless dark. A waystation. A place for ships to stop, to refuel, to resupply.

To dock.

“Kael,” I said. “The Cygnus. Its original mission profile. A Class-4 long-haul. What did it carry?”

He blinked, dragged from his private void. “What? People. Colonists. Biota. Seed banks. The usual.”

“And to support them? To support the ship?”

“Supplies. Spare parts. Fabrication stock.” He waved a limp hand. “It’s all in the dead manifest. Why?”

“The hold we’re in,” I said, my voice tightening. “It’s a cargo hold. But it was reconfigured. For us. For the pods. What was it before?”

Elara was watching me now, the fury in her eyes sharpening into a blade.

Kael frowned, his technician’s mind latching onto a concrete problem, a reprieve from the abstract of our death. “Standard layout for a Class-4… this would have been a secondary cargo bay. For bulk, low-priority items. Raw materials. Possibly…” His eyes went distant, accessing a memory of schematics. “The docking umbilical.”

The words hung in the air.

“The what?” Elara asked.

“A Class-4 doesn’t dock with shuttles,” Kael said, slowly, as if piecing it together himself. “It’s too big. It’s a planet-breaker. But for a deep-space resupply at a corporate waystation… they’d use a rigid umbilical. A telescoping corridor. It extends, latches, creates a sealed pathway. It’s part of the ship. Stored when not in use.” He looked around the vast, dark hold, at the pods glowing like rubies in the black. “It would have been stored here. In a housing along that wall.” He pointed to the far wall, the one stacked with empty crates we’d never bothered to move.

“Is it still there?” My heart was a stone in my throat.

“They reconfigured the hold for the pods,” he said. “They stripped non-essentials. But something that integral, that structurally mounted…” He pushed off from the console, floating toward the crates. “Help me.”

We moved. Elara and I followed, a sudden, desperate gravity pulling us to that wall. We shoved crates aside. They tumbled slowly, soundlessly, in the microgravity. Behind them was the smooth, riveted hull of the ship’s interior. No housing. No controls.

Then Elara found the panel. A square of metal, flush with the wall, painted the same dull grey. Almost invisible. At its center was a small, manual release wheel, stiff with decades of disuse.

Kael grabbed the wheel. He strained. Nothing. Elara put her hands next to his. Their gloves scraped against the metal. I added my weight. For a long moment, nothing. Then, with a shriek that vibrated through the hull and into our bones, the wheel turned a millimeter. Then another. It was like trying to unscrew the world.

It gave. We spun it, a frantic, silent ballet. The panel unsealed with a hiss of equalizing pressure. Behind it was a deep, dark compartment. And inside, folded like the limbs of a dead insect, was a complex assembly of girders and flexible sealant rings. The umbilical.

It was a tool. A piece of infrastructure for the privileged.

“It’s here,” Kael breathed, his voice full of awe and terror. “But it’s useless. It’s a passive system. It needs a matching receiver on the station. A dock. And even if the station has one, we can’t steer the ship. We can’t align. We’re tumbling past.”

I looked at the pulsing green beacon. A function. For the right ship.

“We don’t need to steer the ship,” I said. “We need to steer the dock.”

They both stared at me.

“The beacon is active. The station has power. If it has a dock, it has alignment systems. Thrusters. It’s a waystation. It’s designed to connect to ships that are off-course, damaged.” I was thinking aloud, the logic forming like a crystal in the cold. “The beacon isn’t just a ‘here I am’ signal. It’s a handshake protocol. It’s saying, ‘I am ready to receive.’ Our ship… the Cygnus… it’s the right ship. Its transponder is dead, but its silhouette, its mass signature… the station might see it. Might recognize it as a client.”

“And if it does?” Elara asked.

“Then it should try to connect,” Kael whispered, understanding dawning. “The station’s systems should activate its own docking arm, its positioning thrusters. It would try to match our vector, to latch on.” He looked back at the navigation screen, at our velocity. “But the margin… the force required… it would tear both structures apart. It’s not designed for a catch like this. It’s designed for a gentle rendezvous.”

“We are not a gentle rendezvous,” I said. “We are a stone. But the station is not a window. It is a hand. Maybe a strong one.”

It was a fantasy. A ludicrous fantasy. We were proposing that a centuries-old automated station would see our dead hulk, would burn its precious fuel to chase us down, would perform a maneuver of impossible precision and strength, all to connect a hallway we may or may not be able to open.

It was the only fantasy we had.

“How?” Elara asked, practical to the last. “How do we tell it to try?”

“The beacon is receiving,” Kael said, his mind racing over schematics. “It’s not just a light. It’s a comms laser. Tight-beam. We need to hit it with a signal. A valid signal. A Class-4 docking request.” His face fell. “The main comms array is gone. Blown with the bridge. We have short-range suit radios. Toys.”

I looked at the umbilical housing. At the thick, coiled cables along its frame. “That,” I said. “It’s not just a tunnel. It’s a data conduit. Power. Comms. It has its own external transceiver, for the handshake. Is it… alive?”

Kael dove into the compartment, tracing cables with his gloved fingers. “It’s on the ship’s internal grid. Secondary power… might be intact. The transceiver is a dumb terminal. It needs a signal from the bridge. Or…” He looked up, his eyes wide inside his helmet. “Or from a direct hardline input. A manual override. There’s a port. A physical jack.”

“For what?”

“A diagnostic pad. For engineers. In case the bridge systems failed.” He was scrabbling at a small panel on the umbilical housing. It popped open. Inside was a standard, old-style data socket.

Elara understood first. “Our suit comps. They’re standard issue. They have universal hardline adaptors. For emergency diagnostics on pod systems.”

We were silent. The three of us, floating before the open maw of the housing, looked at each other. We were proposing to plug a personal, low-grade computer—a tool for checking oxygen mixes and battery levels—into the nervous system of a dead starship, to trick a ghost station into catching us.

It was the kind of plan hatched by people who had already deleted everything else.

“We need the docking protocol,” Kael said. “The signal structure. It’s not in my comp. It’s deep in the Cygnus’s core memory. Which is locked behind the bridge firewall.”

I thought of the stone in my hand. A piece of a world. A piece of the ship’s beginning. I thought of the logo, stamped here, stamped there. A brand.

“The main console,” I said. “In the hold. It’s dead, but its memory core might be intact. It’s the same system. It might have the protocol cached. Can you slice it? Extract it?”

Kael stared at me, then at the distant, dark shape of the console across the hold. “It’s a brick. But… the data bus. If I can physically link my comp to its memory board… maybe. It’s a maybe wrapped in a probably not.”

“Do it,” Elara said. Her voice left no room for probably not.

We split. Kael jetted across the hold to the console, pulling tools from his belt. Elara and I remained at the housing. She held the edges of the open panel, her gaze fixed on the pulsing green light in the viewport. It was smaller now. Fainter.

“They designed it to serve them,” she said, not looking at me. “Every piece of this. The ship. The station. The protocol. It was all built to make their journey smoother. To maintain their comfort. Their privilege.” She turned her head, her helmet touching mine lightly. “They never designed it to save us. They designed us to die in it.”

“Then we’ll use it wrong,” I said.

Time became a series of frozen tableaus. Kael, hunched over the disemboweled console, a cable snaking from its guts to his suit’s forearm port. Elara, monitoring the beacon’s pulse, calling out the dwindling signal strength as our distance grew. Me, floating beside the umbilical housing, my hand around the stone, feeling its perfect, useless smoothness.

My mother’s stone. Left behind. Taken. Carried. It had served no purpose. It was weight. But it was a piece of a world that was real. It was an anchor to a time when things grew, and water flowed, and choices were not just between shades of oblivion. It was not a tool. But I was.

“Got it,” Kael’s voice crackled in our helmets. He sounded stunned. “It’s… it’s there. A clean packet. Docking request protocol, Cygnus identifier, all of it. It’s small.”

“Transmit it to me,” I said.

A chime in my helmet. File received. I pulled the hardline cable from my own suit’s port, the connector cold in my grip. I plugged it into the socket inside the umbilical housing.

For a moment, nothing. Then a small, amber light on the housing flickered to life. A dormant system, stirring.

“Manual input recognized,” a flat, synthetic voice said from a hidden speaker. “Awaiting command.”

Elara’s breath was loud in my ear. Kael was a statue across the hold.

I looked at the beacon. A green pulse in the endless black. An open hand, closing.

“Initiate docking sequence,” I said. “Transmit request to target beacon. All parameters. Full authority.”

“Transmitting,” the voice said.

There was no drama. No sound. Just the faint hum of the housing coming to life. On the backup screen, Kael had routed the external sensor feed. We watched the grainy image of the station. The green beacon.

It pulsed.

Once.

Twice.

On the third pulse, it stopped.

The green light went solid.

Then, on the station’s dark hull, a new set of lights ignited. White, harsh, operational lights. They illuminated a gantry, a docking port—a mechanical mouth. We saw small plumes of gas—attitude thrusters—fire along the station’s flanks. It was moving. Turning. Aligning.

“It’s accepted,” Kael whispered, a prayer and a curse. “Gods. It’s coming.”

The station was no longer a silent monument. It was a machine with a purpose. Its thrusters fired in sustained bursts. It was not just aligning. It was pushing. It was altering its own orbit, bleeding momentum, reaching across the void to intercept a tumbling stone.

The physics of it were violent. This was not a docking. It was a capture. The forces involved would be tremendous. The station’s ancient structure, our own battered hull—neither was built for this.

“The pods,” Elara said, her voice urgent. “The children. The G-forces, the shock…”

“We need to dampen the impact,” Kael said, already jetting back toward the main console. “The hold’s emergency buffers. They’re for micrometeor strikes. They might absorb some. If I can route power from the non-existent engines…” His voice trailed off into a mutter of technical incantations.

I stayed at the housing. The amber light was steady. On the screen, the station grew larger. Not because we were approaching it. Because it was approaching us. Faster than I’d imagined. It was a mountain moving with grim, mechanical determination.

A low groan vibrated through the ship. A sound of metal stressed beyond its memory. The station’s docking arm was extending, a black spear tipped with a complex coupling ring. It was reaching for us.

“Brace!” Kael shouted. “I’ve got the buffers online, but it won’t be pretty!”

Elara and I grabbed the sturdy frame of the umbilical housing. I tucked the river stone into the most secure inner pocket of my suit. I did not want to die with it loose in my hand.

The station filled the viewport. Its lights were blinding. The docking arm was a spider’s leg, huge and precise. The coupling ring, spinning slowly to match our tumble, opened like a flower of metal.

It connected.

The sound was not an explosion. It was a fundamental change in the nature of reality. A deep, resonant CLANG-THUD that traveled through every atom of the Cygnus. The ship shuddered, convulsed. The groan became a shriek. Alarms we didn’t know we had began to blare, then were cut off as systems died. The lights in the hold flickered, died, and were replaced by the stark red pulse of emergency lighting.

We were thrown against the housing, then against the wall. My helmet cracked against a girder. Stars bloomed in my vision, not the kind outside. The world was noise and violent, jerking motion. The station’s thrusters were firing at maximum, killing its relative velocity, killing ours. It was a brutal, mechanical embrace.

It lasted a lifetime. It lasted seconds.

Then, a new sound. A series of heavy, definitive THUNKS vibrating up through the deck. Clamps. Magnetic seals. The station had us.

The violent motion ceased, replaced by a profound, absolute stillness. A different stillness than before. Not the stillness of the void. The stillness of being held.

The red lights stabilized. The groaning of tortured metal subsided into creaks, then sighs, then silence.

In the new quiet, the synthetic voice from the housing spoke, calm as a sunlit day.

“Docking sequence complete. Seal integrity verified. Umbilical pressurization in progress.”

A hiss of air came from the depths of the housing. The folded, insectile limbs of the corridor began to unfold, to extend with a smooth, hydraulic purr. Through the viewport, we could see the station’s docking arm, now rigid and true, locked to our hull. A sealed, lit corridor connected us to the angular shadow of the waystation.

We had done it. We had used the master’s tools to break into his abandoned house.

No one moved. No one spoke. The shock was too total.

Kael was the first to break. A sound escaped him, half-laugh, half-sob. He floated away from the console, his limbs loose. Elara pushed off from the wall, drifting toward the mouth of the now-extended umbilical. A soft, white light glowed from within it.

I reached into my pocket. My fingers found the stone. It was still there. Smooth. Cool. A piece of a world. It had not saved us. It had not done anything. But I had carried it here. To this impossible place.

“The pods,” I said, my voice raw. “We need to get them across. Before the ship’s power fails completely.”

Work. Purpose. It was the only way forward.

We worked like machines. Kael and I used the hold’s manual cargo sleds, magnetizing them to the deck to create makeshift gurneys. One by one, we disconnected the sleep pods from their cradles. The red status lights glowed steadily. The children slept on, unaware of the capture, the violence, the salvation. We loaded the pods onto the sleds. Elara led the way, into the umbilical.

It was a tunnel of light. Clean, white panels. Handrails. It felt obscenely normal. It was a hallway built for people in clean clothes, walking to a cafeteria or a lounge. Not for three grimy survivors pushing sleds of frozen hope.

The far end opened into a docking bay of the station. It was vast, empty, and spotlessly clean. The air was cold, sterile, and smelled of ozone and aged metal. Lights overhead flickered on in sequence as we entered, revealing a space that could have held a dozen shuttles. It was utterly deserted. Our footsteps, mag-locked to the deck, echoed in the cathedral silence.

A display panel on the wall lit up as we passed. Text scrolled.

WAYSTATION 7-GAMMA. ORION-CYGNUS ARM. STATUS: STANDBY. ATMOSPHERE: NOMINAL. GRAVITY PLATE ACTIVE. WELCOME, CYGNUS.

The station thought it was greeting its intended client. It was performing its function. For us.

We found a bay near the docking umbilical, a smaller, secondary hangar with power couplings and data ports. A maintenance bay. We carefully lined up the sleep pods, twenty-three ruby lights in the sterile white room. We plugged them into the station’s power grid. One by one, their status lights flickered, then held steady green. They were drawing power from an endless source. Their clocks reset. The countdown to death was canceled.

Elara stood before the pods, her shoulders finally slumping. The ceramic mask shattered. She put a hand against the cold glass of the nearest pod, her head bowing. Kael sank to the deck, his back against a console, and did not move.

I walked away. Down a clean, grey corridor. Past empty crew quarters with names on the doors. Past a silent mess hall with tables still bolted to the floor. Past a viewport that looked out not into the featureless void we’d traveled, but at a sparse, distant spray of stars. We were in a place. A location. We had stopped tumbling.

I found a door labeled OBSERVATION DECK. It slid open with a whisper.

The room was a bubble of transparent alloy. It was furnished with plush, decaying chairs. A corporate logo was etched into the center of the floor—the same insignia. Here, the privileged would have sipped drinks and looked at the stars, a picturesque pause on their journey to a new world.

I walked to the window. I looked back at the Cygnus.

It was a wreck. The entire aft section was a blackened scar. The hull was puckered and torn where the station’s docking clamp had seized it. It looked like a dead thing, caught in the metal jaws of a predator. It was. We had bled it dry, burned its heart, and used its corpse to get here.

I took the river stone from my pocket. I held it up, against the view of the dead ship and the cold stars.

It was the same. Grey. Smooth. A piece of a river that no longer existed, on a world that was dying. It had not changed. It had been carried across an ocean of nothing, through fire and failure and a violence that should have shattered it, to this silent, artificial place.

I had not changed. I was still the person who deleted things. I had deleted my past, my future, my ship. I had tried to delete myself. But I had carried this one, useless thing.

The journey does not change the stone. The stone does not change the journey.

But the hand that carries it is worn by the weight. The eye that sees it learns its contours in every light. The mind that remembers its origin is forced to reconcile that origin with every new, impossible place.

I was not a colonist. I was not a hero. I was salvage. I was a piece of ballast that had, through a series of brutal and illogical events, found itself in the observation lounge.

The class struggle was not over. The station around me was its temple. But I was inside the temple now. And I had twenty-three reasons to learn how its locks worked.

I closed my hand around the stone. It was warm from my grip.

The door behind me hissed open. I did not turn. Elara’s mag-boots clicked on the floor as she walked up to stand beside me. She followed my gaze to the crippled Cygnus.

“Kael is reading the station logs,” she said, her voice quiet in the vast, silent room. “It’s fully automated. Has been for two hundred and forty years. Its reactors are stable. Its fabricators are stocked. There are seed banks in its agricultural sector. Viable.” She paused. “It was a waystation for a colony that never arrived. It has been waiting. Performing its function. For no one.”

“Until now,” I said.

“Until now.”

We stood in silence. The ghosts of the intended were all around us, in the design, in the plush chairs, in the logo on the floor. We were the wrong people in the right place. The ultimate glitch in their system.

“What do we do?” she asked. Not a practical question. A philosophical one.

I looked at the stone in my palm. I thought of my mother, leaving it on a counter. An act without thought. An act that had anchored a life.

“We use it wrong,” I said again. “We wake the children. We teach them how the water reclamation works. We show them how to program the fabricators for clothes that fit, not grey uniforms. We plant the seeds in the hydroponics bays. We make this dead place live.” I turned the stone over. “And we tell them where we came from. And we tell them it was not fair. And we tell them this is not a reward. It is a weapon we took. And they must learn to wield it.”

Elara nodded. She looked at the Cygnus, then at the stars beyond. “The beacon is still active. It’s still calling. In case another right ship comes.”

I followed her gaze. The green light was invisible from here, but I knew it was there. A function. A hand held out in the dark.

“Let it call,” I said.

I am deleting nothing else.

The stone lives in my palm. It is my single point of frictionless, illogical weight. It is the anchor for everything that comes next.

END OF CHAPTER 24

HOOK FOR CHAPTER 25: In the station’s core log, Kael finds an entry, not automated. A final, fragmented manual update from a station chief, dated ten years after the sky turned. It reads: “They’re not coming. The project is dead. We are the last. Initiated long-range scan on the target colony world, per contingency. Received a single, automated ping back. Coordinates attached. It’s not dead. Something is there. Something is awake. Do not follow. Repeat, do not—” The transmission cuts off. The attached coordinates are not for a dead world. They are for a different sector entirely. And the station’s long-range navigation system, now powered and intact, is already plotting the course.
